    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17569409253314955000"><i>Platero y yo</i> es una narración de <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36635.Juan_Ram_n_Jim_nez' title='Juan Ramón Jiménez' rel='nofollow noopener'>Juan Ramón Jiménez</a> que recrea poéticamente la vida y muerte del burro Platero. Es muy celebre el primer parrafo: “Platero es pequeño, peludo, suave; tan blando por fuera, que se diría todo de algodón, que no lleva huesos. Sólo los espejos de azabache de sus ojos son duros cual dos escarabajos de cristal negro. Lo dejo suelto y se va al prad</span>
  <span id="freeText17569409253314955000" style="display:none"><i>Platero y yo</i> es una narración de <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36635.Juan_Ram_n_Jim_nez" title="Juan Ramón Jiménez" rel="nofollow noopener">Juan Ramón Jiménez</a> que recrea poéticamente la vida y muerte del burro Platero. Es muy celebre el primer parrafo: “Platero es pequeño, peludo, suave; tan blando por fuera, que se diría todo de algodón, que no lleva huesos. Sólo los espejos de azabache de sus ojos son duros cual dos escarabajos de cristal negro. Lo dejo suelto y se va al prado y acaricia tibiamente, rozándolas apenas, las florecillas rosas, celestes y gualdas... Lo llamo dulcemente: ¿Platero?, y viene a mi con un trotecillo alegre, que parece que se ríe, en no sé que cascabeleo ideal...”<br /><br />La primera edición se publico en 1914 (Ediciones De La Lectura) y en 1917 se publicó la edición completa, compuesta por 138 capítulos (Editorial Calleja, Madrid). Quedaba claro que era un texto adulto, aunque por su sencillez y transparencia se adecuara perfectamente a la imaginación y al gusto de los niños. Algunos capítulos encerraban una cierta critica social, revelando una dimensión del autor que muchos tardaron en advertir. El propio <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36635.Juan_Ram_n_Jim_nez" title="Juan Ramón Jiménez" rel="nofollow noopener">Juan Ramón Jiménez</a>, en un prologuillo a la edición aclaraba: “Yo nunca he escrito ni escribiré nada para niños porque creo que el niño puede leer los libros que lee el hombre, con determinadas excepciones que a todos se le ocurren.”<br /><br />El poeta tenía la intención de ampliar el texto hasta los 190 capítulos; de hecho, existen tres adicionales, escritos en la década de 1920. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36635.Juan_Ram_n_Jim_nez" title="Juan Ramón Jiménez" rel="nofollow noopener">Juan Ramón Jiménez</a> planeó también una segunda parte, denominada <i>Otra vida de Platero</i>, de la que incluso esbozó algunos títulos. Un proyecto que, como el de publicar <i>Platero y yo</i> en cuadernos sueltos, no llegaría nunca a ver la luz.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17569409253314955000" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div id="review_1529331521" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1529331521" />
    <a title="Debbie Zapata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22110057-debbie-zapata"><img alt="Debbie Zapata" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451631863p2/22110057.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1529331521?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie Zapata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie Zapata" href="/user/show/22110057-debbie-zapata">Debbie Zapata</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19390339-platero-y-yo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1529331521" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8458239012172031678"><b>Edited on August 30, 2016</b><br/><br/>Well, I have been chipping away at this as promised below the line and I have finally finished. It helped a lot that on my last trip to Arizona I packed my English language edition of the book. I still read the Spanish language edition but instead of the dictionary I was able to read the translations of the many many poems and I understood it all much better that way. <br/><br/>Did I enjoy it? Yes and no. There were plenty of vivid images, and Platero was quite the character. But </span>
  <span id="freeText8458239012172031678" style="display:none"><b>Edited on August 30, 2016</b><br /><br />Well, I have been chipping away at this as promised below the line and I have finally finished. It helped a lot that on my last trip to Arizona I packed my English language edition of the book. I still read the Spanish language edition but instead of the dictionary I was able to read the translations of the many many poems and I understood it all much better that way. <br /><br />Did I enjoy it? Yes and no. There were plenty of vivid images, and Platero was quite the character. But overall it was depressing, and I found myself fighting off the blues if I read too many pages at one sitting. <br /><br />A classic, yes. A Pulitzer Prize winner, yes again. But a delight that has captured hearts? This is what the back cover of my English edition claims. Perhaps Platero captured other hearts, but mine is still free.<br /><br />~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~<br />Okay, first I want to say that I am not marking this book DNF. I am just saving it for later. Really!<br /><br />You see, it is simply too much work for me to read the rest of this right now. Jimenez uses words that are so unfamiliar to me that I have spent more time with my Spanish/English dictionary than I have with Platero. That is not a bad thing in itself, but these days I have a lot going on in real life and the need to Hunt and Peck rather than Read is becoming a huge annoyance. <br /><br />I have liked what I have read so far (I am at chapter 21 of 63....see how slowly this is going?!) but I am simply not in a good place mentally to study anymore. I need to be entertained just now, not be in school.<br /><br />So I have created a new bookshelf called Mas Tarde (Later) and I am stabling Platero there for awhile. I will chip away at it now and then, and when I finish I will edit this review and rate the book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8458239012172031678" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_99580512" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99580512" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/99580512?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780292764798" href="/book/show/1082366.Platero_and_I">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2010 edition)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99580512" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3010396778000193994">Juan Ramon Jimenez (1881-1958) was a Spanish poet who won the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1956. He mostly wrote erotic poems but critics say that they were in "true" French form. However, in 1914, he gave tribute to his hometown (an Andalusian village of Moguer in the south of Spain) with this beautiful book, <b>Platero and I: An Andalusian Elegy</b>. Originally written in Spanish, it is about a friendship between a man and his donkey. However, just like in any other well-loved children's books, this</span>
  <span id="freeText3010396778000193994" style="display:none">Juan Ramon Jimenez (1881-1958) was a Spanish poet who won the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1956. He mostly wrote erotic poems but critics say that they were in "true" French form. However, in 1914, he gave tribute to his hometown (an Andalusian village of Moguer in the south of Spain) with this beautiful book, <b>Platero and I: An Andalusian Elegy</b>. Originally written in Spanish, it is about a friendship between a man and his donkey. However, just like in any other well-loved children's books, this story can be read in two levels: a child can read it as is and enjoy the many adventures and misadventures of the man and his donkey, <b>Platero</b> or an adult can read it as one big metaphor.<br /><br />The story is told by the author mostly addressing his words directly to Platero. However, he starts the story by describing his pet: <i>Platero is small, downy, smooth - so soft to the touch that one would think he were all cotton, that he had no bones. Only the jet mirrors of his eyes are hard as two beetles of dark crystal.</i> With this description, Platero seems not to be a donkey at all. Checking Wikipedia, Platero actually symbolizes the simple, rustic and laidback village where Jimenez spent his childhood. He wrote <i>Platero and I</i> upon his return from Madrid where he met the Latin American Poet Ruben Dario who he later replaced as the leading poet in Spanish-speaking countries during that time. The popular then poet Jimenez, for an unknown reason, retreated back to his hometown and spent six years (1905-1911) missing his childhood and the things that were associated with it.<br /><br />His prose in this book is almost like poetry. I particularly enjoyed <b>The Boneyard</b> where Jimenez describes his plan in case Platero dies. Jimenez makes it an introduction to the elegiac mood that dominates this book. Being an erotic poet, is there anything erotic in this supposedly children's book? Only this part where the man and Platero are watching a nearby open fire:<i>
  <blockquote>"Don't you like fire, Platero? I don't think that the body of any nude woman can be compared with fire. What flowing hair, what arms, what legs could stand comparison with these fiery nudities? Nature has perhaps no better offering than fire."</blockquote>
</i> I really liked this book. Easy read. Like reading short poems. Full of symbolisms. Reminds me of my childhood in our small provincial town. Reminds me of the things I used to enjoy there. When life was a lot simpler. When there are christmas carols playing during early December mornings. This book, <i>Platero and I</i> almost rivals my favorite book in the genre, Antoine de Saint-Exupery's <i>The Little Prince</i>. <br /><br />If I were a young man reading this for the first time, I would right away still prefer Saint-Exupery's work for its cohesiveness and structure. <i>Platero and I</i> seems to be fragmented and feels like a sad long lamentations of an dying man and what makes it worse is that in the end, he is lamenting for a dead donkey. At least, in Saint-Exupery's tale, towards the end, the young little prince is missing his rose that is still fresh and smelling nice.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3010396778000193994" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1726580786" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1726580786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13962350190476751881">Platero in the title is narrator's pet donkey. Jimenez sometimes talks to him sometimes talks about him in these prose poems either talking about memories (real? no idea) of experiences they had in each-other's company - simple pleasures, children, animals or cruel tragedies (death of people or animals, old age etc).<br/><br/>One of the personal favorite chapters:<br/><b>The White Mare</b><br/><i><br/>"I have come home sad, Platero. Look: as I was coming through the Calle de las Flores, already at the Portada, in the very site wh</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13962350190476751881" style="display:none">Platero in the title is narrator's pet donkey. Jimenez sometimes talks to him sometimes talks about him in these prose poems either talking about memories (real? no idea) of experiences they had in each-other's company - simple pleasures, children, animals or cruel tragedies (death of people or animals, old age etc).<br /><br />One of the personal favorite chapters:<br /><b>The White Mare</b><br /><i><br />"I have come home sad, Platero. Look: as I was coming through the Calle de las Flores, already at the Portada, in the very site where lightning killed the twin children, the white mare of El Sordo lay dead. Some almost naked children stood silently about. Purita, the seamstress who was passing by, told me that El Sordo, sick and tired of feeding the mare, had taken her to the boneyard this morning. You know that the poor old thing was as old as Don Julian and as stupid. She could not see or hear, and could scarcely walk. At about noon, the mare appeared again at the entrance to her master's house. Irritated, he seized a vine prop and tried to drive her away with blows. She would not go. Then he struck her with a sickle. People came up and amid curses and joking, the mare set out up the street, limping and stumbling. The children fol­lowed her with shouts and stones. Finally she fell to the ground and they finished killing her there. Some expression of pity, such as: "Let her die in peace!" hovered over her as if you and I had been there, Platero; but it was like a butterfly whirling in a strong sea wind. The stones were still lying beside her when I saw her, she as cold as they. One of her eyes was wide open. Sightless while she was alive, now that she was dead, it seemed to see. Its whiteness was the only light re­maining in that dark street, over which the evening sky, seeming very high in the cold, was covered with the lightest fleece of pink clouds. "</i></span>

<div id="review_280853523" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/280853523" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/280853523?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780451503022" href="/book/show/11904424-platero-and-i">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=classic-fiction">classic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=folk-fairy-tales">folk-fairy-tales</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=nature-animals">nature-animals</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer280853523" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer252360703303345202">This book is a collection of vignettes, most a scant few paragraphs each, about a man's affection for his beloved donkey and his love for the world around him. <br/><br/>Jimenez was a renowned poet and won the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1956. And though this book is written in prose, the writing sings like pure poetry:<br/><br/><i>The clear wind from the sea sweeps up the red slope to the field at the summit and breaks into laughter among the tender white flowers...<br/><br/>The well! What a deep word, Platero, so dark a gre</i></span>
  <span id="freeText252360703303345202" style="display:none">This book is a collection of vignettes, most a scant few paragraphs each, about a man's affection for his beloved donkey and his love for the world around him. <br /><br />Jimenez was a renowned poet and won the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1956. And though this book is written in prose, the writing sings like pure poetry:<br /><br /><i>The clear wind from the sea sweeps up the red slope to the field at the summit and breaks into laughter among the tender white flowers...<br /><br />The well! What a deep word, Platero, so dark a green, so cool, so resonant! It seems as if it were the word itself which spun and bored into the dark earth until it struck water.<br /><br />Look how the setting sun, displaying itself like a visible god, draws to itself every ecstasy and sinks into the thin line of sea behind Huelva...</i><br /><br />And have you ever heard fireworks described quite so beautifully?<br /><br /><i>Oh, what flaming peacocks, what celestial clusters of bright roses, what fiery pheasants in the gardens of the stars!</i><br /><br />A few of the sketches deal sensitively with the deaths of children and aged animals, but most of the book is a celebration of the simple joys of being alive.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="252360703303345202" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText8023510203816011660" style="display:none">Night is falling, already foggy and purple. Vague patches of mauve and green brightness linger behind the church tower. You can't have any idea of how one's deep breathing expands one's chest when, arriving up there by the little dark wooden staircase, a person feels scorched in the full sunlight of the day, drowned in azure as if alongside heaven itself, and blinded by the gleam of the whitewash, which, as you know, is applied to the brick surface so that the rainwater will arrive clean in the cistern.<br />*<br />The clear sea breeze climbs the red slope, reaches the meadow at the summit, laughs among the tender white flowers, then becomes entangled in the uncleared pine scrub and, puffing them out like thin sails, rocks the blazing sky-blue, pink, and gold cobwebs...By now the whole afternoon is sea breeze. And the sun and breeze make the heart feel soft and comfortable!<br />*<br />Night is falling, and the moon flares at the bottom there, adorned with fickle stars. Silence! Along the roads life has departed far...</span>

<div id="review_790291258" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/790291258" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/790291258?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9782232113239" href="/book/show/5977771-platero-and-i">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=3-star">3-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=nobel-prize-people">nobel-prize-people</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2017">r-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer790291258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15157121870898910470">I read this purely out of Nobel Prize for Lit reputation incentive, which in hindsight would not have been as persuasive as it was without a Latinx translation appended to the description (Jiménez is from Spain, though, so the first of that doesn't adhere). I had heard things about reclaimed childhood and masterful prose and thought I could use a break from revisiting old politically savvy and narratologically complicated works by trying out something new and laid back instead. I certainly got w</span>
  <span id="freeText15157121870898910470" style="display:none">I read this purely out of Nobel Prize for Lit reputation incentive, which in hindsight would not have been as persuasive as it was without a Latinx translation appended to the description (Jiménez is from Spain, though, so the first of that doesn't adhere). I had heard things about reclaimed childhood and masterful prose and thought I could use a break from revisiting old politically savvy and narratologically complicated works by trying out something new and laid back instead. I certainly got what I came for, to the point that I would recommend this as suitable bedtime story material for the child keen on pictures and the parent on early cosmopolitan influence. The categories of metaphor the work utilizes are, however, stagnant, and the book would not be worth passing down without critical attention paid to depictions of Romani and women and mental illness, which begs the question of when it's appropriate to start such conversations. Leave it too long and you get the sort of sadists passing for adults these days, so something must be done.<br /><br />The reason why I offer this to children is due to how concrete a picture it offers of a place so often mythologized by the "lost" generation and other non-Spanish Europeanizations. Spain straddles an unusual border of fetishization wherein its settler states are otherized in contrast to the Anglo norm while its denizens are not, so it's worth inculcating a perspective towards the motherland that's more fleshed out than tales of the Spanish Civil War and "illegal" immigration. The language is also lush enough to evocatively make its way through translation, so it, as well as the pictures, would make for a quality reading experience for those still mediating between image and symbol, especially if those conducting the reading are willing to go the extra step of lessons in history and geography. Again, though, if one wishes to avoid the vacuum, one must go all the way, and not leave a child to decide whether or not to be pressured into thinking of human beings as a series of check boxes, to the emptiness of which one must react with violence. The 19th and 20th centuries of the author's time did much to both build up and break down these boxes, and it remains to be seen how the 21st century will pan out.<br /><br />Short and temperate this was, and so I'm ready to return to more convoluted climes. Unlikely as I am to have children, borne or adopted, I can well imagine myself being the weird aunt who plagues my sister's doorstep with odd gifts and odder bedtime stories. As such, this work is one I'l be keeping in mind, qualifiers and all, for as said by one whose name I can't recall, a book given to a child must be worth given to an adult (in the most worthy sense of the word "adult), else it does a disservice to both.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15157121870898910470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_172073613" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172073613" />
    <a title="Vasha7" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3850379-vasha7"><img alt="Vasha7" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172073613?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vasha7" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vasha7" href="/user/show/3850379-vasha7">Vasha7</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9782232113239" href="/book/show/5977771-platero-and-i">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172073613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15934354292177607864">A poet (Juan Ramón Jiménez), a donkey (Platero), a town in the Spanish countryside (Moguer). The author wanders with Platero, eating figs and quinces from the trees, resting in the shade, observing the animals and the people (mostly poor and ragged), and finding beauty everywhere. He describes what he sees in short chapters of rich language. He addresses most of his remarks to Platero, his best friend. Perhaps because the donkey's innocence helps him see innocently.<br/><br/>The author seems to have money</span>
  <span id="freeText15934354292177607864" style="display:none">A poet (Juan Ramón Jiménez), a donkey (Platero), a town in the Spanish countryside (Moguer). The author wanders with Platero, eating figs and quinces from the trees, resting in the shade, observing the animals and the people (mostly poor and ragged), and finding beauty everywhere. He describes what he sees in short chapters of rich language. He addresses most of his remarks to Platero, his best friend. Perhaps because the donkey's innocence helps him see innocently.<br /><br />The author seems to have money but isn't one of the town's respected citizens; the children run after him shouting "Crazy-man!" He believes in Heaven but doubts that you can get there by kneeling in a dark church listening to a grey priest, and therefore stays away on Sundays. The one thing the Catholic church provides that he really appreciates is processions, with colorful banners, fancy costumes, flowers, music, and everything. Black donkeys, ravens, and pitch-darkness give him the creeps. He dislikes large groups of people and loves children; children are everywhere in this book.<br /><br />I like the chapter "The Locked Gate" -- it's so true that looking through a gate at fields and a road you've never taken makes them seem wonderful; it's no wonder that the poet never goes through the gate even though it's not actually locked. There's also a chapter about the effect of seeing and hearing ordinary things from the height of a rooftop. All in how you see it. Sunlight is always changing its effect according to the time of day, the season, or whether you look at it hazily illuminating the depths of a pool or speckling the leaves of a tree.<br /><br />I didn't have a clue what to expect when I began reading this book, so it's a delightful surprise.<br /><br />Translated by Eloïse Roach; with drawings by Jo Alys Downs.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15934354292177607864" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2008125998" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2008125998" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2008125998?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=spanish-lit">spanish-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2008125998" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3493812473869452017">Platero y yo is a work similar in spirit to a tradition practiced by Poles on December 24. For our vigil supper (Wigilia), we put the best hay that we can find under the tablecloth in the hope that the prayers said during the meal will bless the hay. After the meal is finished before leaving for the Shepherd's mass at midnight we give the hay to the animals in the barn (if such we have). The point of the ritual is to remind us that as Christians we have a duty to love and respect those animals i</span>
  <span id="freeText3493812473869452017" style="display:none">Platero y yo is a work similar in spirit to a tradition practiced by Poles on December 24. For our vigil supper (Wigilia), we put the best hay that we can find under the tablecloth in the hope that the prayers said during the meal will bless the hay. After the meal is finished before leaving for the Shepherd's mass at midnight we give the hay to the animals in the barn (if such we have). The point of the ritual is to remind us that as Christians we have a duty to love and respect those animals in our care. If we do so, we will receive the same in return.<br /><br />Platero y yo contains a very mature reflection on the relationship between a man and his donkey. Platero is no anthropomorphic animal. With all his goodness and loyalty Platero remains a donkey. Jimenez despite his great love for Platero never has any illusions about Platero's nature. As a piece of literature, Platero y yo is sweet but in no way foolish.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3493812473869452017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7850749405780076605">A rare gift of a book, a magical tour de force about the close friendship between a man and his donkey. Heartbreaking and mesmerizing at once, the story is set in a small rural town in Spain in the early part of the twentieth century. The linked poems, originally written in Spanish, have been translated into many languages.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14620577609529916789">Platero and I is one of those books you read as a child in school but they remain in your head forever. When I was writing this review I have reread the beginning of the book and suddenly I had a great desire to read it again.   <span id="freeText14620577609529916789" style="display:none">Platero and I is one of those books you read as a child in school but they remain in your head forever. When I was writing this review I have reread the beginning of the book and suddenly I had a great desire to read it again. Platero and I is an excellent book, with a tender and original story about a donkey's life (from birth to death). We are used to read books where the main characters are human but sometimes animals, especially Platero, has an equally or more interesting life than many of us! ;)<br /><br />I leave you with the initial fragment of the novel (in Spanish) by Juan Ramon Jimenez:<br /><br />"Platero es pequeño, peludo, suave; tan blando por fuera, que se diría todo de algodón, que no lleva huesos. Sólo los espejos de azabache de sus ojos son duros cual dos escarabajos de cristal negro. Lo dejo suelto y se va al prado y acaricia tibiamente, rozándolas apenas, las florecillas rosas, celestes y gualdas... Lo llamo dulcemente: ¿Platero?, y viene a mí con un trotecillo alegre, que parece que se ríe, en no sé qué cascabeleo ideal..."<br /><br /><br />Spanish version:<br />Platero y yo es de esos libros que lees de pequeño en la escuela pero se quedan grabados de por vida en la cabeza. Escribiendo esta review he vuelto a leer el inicio del libro y me han entrado unas ganas enormes de volver a leerlo. Platero y yo es un libro excelente, con una historia tierna y tan original como contar la vida de un burro desde su nacimiento hasta su muerte. Estamos acostumbrados a que los personajes protagonistas de los libros que leemos sean humanos pero hay animales, y en especial Platero, que tienen vida igual o más interesantes que muchos de nosotros! ;)<br /><br />Os dejo con el fragmento inicial de la novela de Juan Ramón Jiménez:<br /><br />"Platero es pequeño, peludo, suave; tan blando por fuera, que se diría todo de algodón, que no lleva huesos. Sólo los espejos de azabache de sus ojos son duros cual dos escarabajos de cristal negro. Lo dejo suelto y se va al prado y acaricia tibiamente, rozándolas apenas, las florecillas rosas, celestes y gualdas... Lo llamo dulcemente: ¿Platero?, y viene a mí con un trotecillo alegre, que parece que se ríe, en no sé qué cascabeleo ideal..."<br /></span>

<div id="review_2504189041" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2504189041" />
    <a title="Monty Milne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14797192-monty-milne"><img alt="Monty Milne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363450934p2/14797192.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2504189041?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monty Milne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monty Milne" href="/user/show/14797192-monty-milne">Monty Milne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9787543673137" href="/book/show/39327922-platero-and-i">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2504189041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6476108570590695723">In general, the further south one travels in Europe, the kinder people are to children and the crueller they are to animals. The patient suffering of donkeys in the Latin world is legendary. One acknowledges that over time there has been an amelioration. I cannot forget the horror, on reading Sir Charles Oman's History of the Peninsular War, of the passage where Wellington's army find hundreds of donkeys left behind by the retreating French, all of which have been cruelly hamstrung and abandoned</span>
  <span id="freeText6476108570590695723" style="display:none">In general, the further south one travels in Europe, the kinder people are to children and the crueller they are to animals. The patient suffering of donkeys in the Latin world is legendary. One acknowledges that over time there has been an amelioration. I cannot forget the horror, on reading Sir Charles Oman's History of the Peninsular War, of the passage where Wellington's army find hundreds of donkeys left behind by the retreating French, all of which have been cruelly hamstrung and abandoned in agony in case they are used by the enemy. And a nineteenth century pope once said that it is not a sin to starve a dog or beat it to death. But despite the brutalities of the bullfight, even the Spanish are not so cruel now, perhaps?<br /><br />There is plenty of evidence of cruelty to both man and beast in this novel, but it is also an evocative reminder that cruelty is not the unremitting norm. The narrator's love for his burro is beautifully done, and one hopes and senses that it was and is more common than the indifference and cruelty with which such burros have been too often treated. But this wonderful little novel is far more than just an account of the bond between boy and burro: it is a meditation on the beauty of the Spanish landscape, the passage of time, and the mysteries of love and death. All of it expressed in the most marvellous poetic language. And all of this without falling into either sentimentality or anthropomorphism. I have not read the Spanish original, but if this excellent translation is as faithful as I suspect it to be, then truly this is a sparkling gem in a dark world.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6476108570590695723" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2566787621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5843089120125232586">Platero and I was written in the first decade of the twentieth century and reveals a life which has almost completely died out. The poet tells stories of, and too, his close companion, his donkey Platero.<br/><br/>There is nostalgia in the author's stories but this does not make the book sentimental. The life described here, of the people in the town of Moguer in Andalusia, is a simple traditional country one where the weather and the seasons are key guides, where a dog rescuing her puppies is worthy of a</span>
  <span id="freeText5843089120125232586" style="display:none">Platero and I was written in the first decade of the twentieth century and reveals a life which has almost completely died out. The poet tells stories of, and too, his close companion, his donkey Platero.<br /><br />There is nostalgia in the author's stories but this does not make the book sentimental. The life described here, of the people in the town of Moguer in Andalusia, is a simple traditional country one where the weather and the seasons are key guides, where a dog rescuing her puppies is worthy of a tale and the church and religious festivals are important cultural markers. But the author describes also the harshness of the life and its cruelties - death, poverty and animal cruelty.<br /><br />The language is beautiful, and each chapter is a little vignette adding to the overall story. The book is poignant, sad, meaningful and I highly recommend it. My edition also has beautiful black and white drawings by Baltasar Lobo but I believe it is not fully complete. I hope to read the full version one day. I cannot imagine parting with this beautiful edition though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5843089120125232586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_22511984" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22511984" />
    <a title="Maria  Caporale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1168439-maria-caporale"><img alt="Maria  Caporale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1211030177p2/1168439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22511984" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2292948616889803389">This book gave such a wonderful picture of Spain in that era. The innocence of the children, the beauty of nature and the bonding and love a person has with their animal. I love Platero who is so soft, he feels like he has no bones when you hug him; yet he is so tough, hard-working and strong. The short essays are tremendously sweet and beautifully descriptive. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

These wonderful prose poems cover a year of insights and observations that Juan Ramon Jimenez made while recuperating in Andalusia from depression after his father died. Roach's version has some delightful drawings by Jo Alys Downs that add to the over all book.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12822631259438230505">Maude. I tried, I really did. The language is very poetic and beautiful but I swear I’d read a paragraph and by the next one I’d lose all sense of what was going on. There’s no plot and not even many details about anyone but the donkey and the town. At the beginning I assumed the narrator was a very old and lonely man, with no one but his donkey and then it becomes clear he has a wife and kids. It also feels like a far older setting than it is - Mother Theresa gets mentioned and it turns out sma</span>
  <span id="freeText12822631259438230505" style="display:none">Maude. I tried, I really did. The language is very poetic and beautiful but I swear I’d read a paragraph and by the next one I’d lose all sense of what was going on. There’s no plot and not even many details about anyone but the donkey and the town. At the beginning I assumed the narrator was a very old and lonely man, with no one but his donkey and then it becomes clear he has a wife and kids. It also feels like a far older setting than it is - Mother Theresa gets mentioned and it turns out small Spanish villages in the fifties were just really not modern in any way yet. <br /><br />The thing is that I read a children’s shortened version of this only a few months ago and yet none of the sections were at all familiar this time. It really was all in one eye and straight out of my memory apparently. I only finished it because it’s a 1001 book. <br /><br />And then the damn donkey dies!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9481565743895723264">I may have found my poetry place... for example:<br/><br/><i><b>93. Dawn</b><br/> In the slow-coming mornings of winter, when the watchful roosters see the first roses of dawn and give them gallant greeting, Platero, tired of sleep, brays for a long time. How sweet is his distant awakening, in the sky-blue light which streaks through the cracks in my bedroom. I, also eager for the day, think of the sun from the softness of my bed.<br/> And I think of what would have become of poor Platero if, instead of falling into my poet'</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9481565743895723264" style="display:none">I may have found my poetry place... for example:<br /><br /><i><b>93. Dawn</b><br /> In the slow-coming mornings of winter, when the watchful roosters see the first roses of dawn and give them gallant greeting, Platero, tired of sleep, brays for a long time. How sweet is his distant awakening, in the sky-blue light which streaks through the cracks in my bedroom. I, also eager for the day, think of the sun from the softness of my bed.<br /> And I think of what would have become of poor Platero if, instead of falling into my poet's hands, he had fallen into the hands of one of those charcoal-makers who go while it is still dark along the hard frost of deserted paths to steal pine in the forest, or into the hands of one of those miserable gypsies who paint their donkeys and feed them arsenic and put needles in their ears to keep them from falling.<br /> Platero brays again. Can he know that I am thinking of him? What does it matter to me? In the softness of the early light the thought of him is as pleasing to me as the dawn. And I thank God that he has a stall as warm and soft as a crib, as kindly as the thoughts I have of him.</i></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer919766065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1810675929568395646">Subtitle of the book is The Elegy. <i>Platero and I</i> consists of 141 narrative prose poems addressed to the poet's donkey. First published 1914. This Shambhala edition from 1978 is translated by Antonio T. de Nicolas.<br/><br/>Lush, rich images piled one on top of the other. Did Christopher Dewdney read Jimenez? Ron Silliman has characterized Dewdney's lush imagery as pornographic, I think; if so, then Jimenez's is the opposite of that. They are both extraordinarily denotative in their use of the image (this </span>
  <span id="freeText1810675929568395646" style="display:none">Subtitle of the book is The Elegy. <i>Platero and I</i> consists of 141 narrative prose poems addressed to the poet's donkey. First published 1914. This Shambhala edition from 1978 is translated by Antonio T. de Nicolas.<br /><br />Lush, rich images piled one on top of the other. Did Christopher Dewdney read Jimenez? Ron Silliman has characterized Dewdney's lush imagery as pornographic, I think; if so, then Jimenez's is the opposite of that. They are both extraordinarily denotative in their use of the image (this appears the case even for Dewdney's Olivia Butleresque scifi <i>Permugenesis</i>, for example), expanding the world by exalting the literal, rather than sealing the world off inside a handful of symbols. Robert "Iron Man" Bly translated Jimenez in the sixties, probably on reconnaissance for his "deep image" (wonder if Bly thought he found any).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1810675929568395646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5982708481836423288" style="display:none">These endearing stories by Spanish Nobel laureate Juan Ramon Jimenez are a series of monologues between a man and his donkey (Platero); as well as with Diana the Goat and a parrot who's only words are "Ce n'est rien" (it is nothing). These stories are really something! I also recommend reading Juan Ramon Jimenez's selected poems &amp; prose, "Light and Shadows." The following is one of my favorites "Even though my soul fits so wonderfully inside my body - like a clear idea in a line perfect for it - nevertheless it has to abandon the body eventually, leaving it like some academic's line, hollow and stiff!"</span>

<div id="review_2737264031" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2737264031" />
    <a title="Karla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11423141-karla"><img alt="Karla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343862447p2/11423141.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2737264031?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karla" href="/user/show/11423141-karla">Karla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2737264031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17880802373296150088">A sweet ode to a town and donkey in the southwest of Spain, this book is a series of one to two page vignettes between a man and his donkey, Platero. A Nobel Prize winner for his poetry, the small musings here are quite poetic. Cleverly he has written them as for children, but with a lot of symbolism and metaphors for adults to enjoy. Being written long ago, a few of the characters are written stereotypically. Sadly the flow of the words comes to a halt when you come upon misogynistic or racist </span>
  <span id="freeText17880802373296150088" style="display:none">A sweet ode to a town and donkey in the southwest of Spain, this book is a series of one to two page vignettes between a man and his donkey, Platero. A Nobel Prize winner for his poetry, the small musings here are quite poetic. Cleverly he has written them as for children, but with a lot of symbolism and metaphors for adults to enjoy. Being written long ago, a few of the characters are written stereotypically. Sadly the flow of the words comes to a halt when you come upon misogynistic or racist terms. One can blame the time, though even with a lack of enmity, it still clangs in your ears. If you can get through the handful of these, the rest of the book is quirky and sweet, much like Platero.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17880802373296150088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_144611'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/144611.Campos_de_Castilla"><img alt="Campos de Castilla" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1172164540l/144611._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MJZASrTxYA7cIjvUFFWsMebnnjAUvEpp+JCugJSW1UDZ8GHZhOzRFhHygirDEtlV+y0K5COi+/nR54q9u0+8Ww==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1D3q6/ngA6cw2kHLhOyG4SwR8SH5flU8hcStIM+hsv09W8t4yf2yv/0K+DVTq/OFMdtl9c5g5Kyss4kd4Hjb5g==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/950051-platero-y-yo">Quotes from Platero y yo</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Yo nunca he escrito ni escribiré nada para niños, porque creo que el niño puede leer los libros que lee el hombre, con determinadas excepciones que a todos se le ocurren. También habrá excepciones para hombres y para mujeres.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/795775-yo-nunca-he-escrito-ni-escribir-nada-para-ni-os-porque">3 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Asnografía<br /><br /> Leo en un Diccionario: ASNOGRAFÍA, s.f.: Se dice, irónicamente, por descripción del asno. <br /><br /> ¡Pobre asno! ¡Tan bueno, tan noble, tan agudo como eres ! Irónicamente... ¿Por qué? ¿Ni una descripción seria mereces, tú, cuya descripción cierta sería un cuento de primavera? ¡Si al hombre que es bueno debieran decirle asno! ¡Si al asno que es malo debieran decirle hombre! <br />Irónicamente... De ti, tan intelectual, amigo del viejo y del niño, del arroyo y de la mariposa, del sol y del perro, de la flor y de la luna, paciente y reflexivo, melancólico y amable, Marco Aurelio de los prados...<br /><br /> Platero, que sin duda comprende, me mira fijamente con sus ojazos lucientes, de una blanda dureza, en los que el sol brilla, pequeñito y chispeante en un breve y convexo firmamento verdinegro. ¡Ay! ¡Si su peluda cabezota idílica supiera que yo le hago justicia, que yo soy mejor que esos hombres que escriben Diccionarios, casi tan bueno como él!<br /><br /> Y he puesto al margen del libro: ASNOGRAFÍA, sentido figurado: Se debe decir, con ironía, ¡claro está!, por descripción del hombre imbécil que escribe Diccionarios.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/9015555-asnograf-a-leo-en-un-diccionario-asnograf-a-s-f-se-dice-ir-nicamente">2 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
